## Week 6.2

### 1. _Schenckâ€¯v.â€¯Unitedâ€¯States_,â€¯249â€¯U.S.â€¯47â€¯(1919)

> **Factsâ€¯/â€¯Proceduralâ€¯Posture**  
> â€“â€¯Springâ€¯1917: **Charlesâ€¯Schenck** (Gen.â€¯Secretary, Socialist Party of America) and **Elizabethâ€¯Baer** mail out 15,000 leaflets urging recently drafted men to â€œassert your rightsâ€ and **resist conscription** under the new Selective Service Act.  
> â€“â€¯Indicted in the **E.D.â€¯Pa.** for violating **Â§â€¯3 of the Espionage Act of 1917** (willfully causing or attempting to cause insubordination or obstruction of enlistment).  
> â€“â€¯Jury convicts; Judge Clayton imposes 6â€‘month sentences. Circuit Court of Appeals affirms (per curiam). SCOTUS grants expedited cert.; argument Jan.â€¯9, decision Mar.â€¯3â€¯1919â€”**the same Term as _Debs_ and _Frohwerk_.**

> **Governmentâ€™s Theory (U.S. Attorney & Solicitor General Lamar)**  
> â€“â€¯The leaflet was an **intentional attempt to obstruct the draft**, a vital war measure; when â€œthe nation is at war, many things that might be said in time of peace are a hindrance.â€  
> â€“â€¯**No prior restraint** involvedâ€”only retrospective punishment for criminal attempt.  
> â€“â€¯First Amendment doesnâ€™t protect speech that **creates a substantive evil Congress may prevent**.

> **Defendantsâ€™ Theory (Schenck & Baer)**  
> â€“â€¯Leaflet advocates **peaceful political opposition**â€”petitioning to repeal the draftâ€”not sabotage.  
> â€“â€¯Espionage Act, as applied, punishes **expression of opinion** and is therefore **repugnant to the First Amendment**.  
> â€“â€¯No evidence that the circulars **actually interfered** with enlistment.

> **Issues**
> 
> 1. Does punishing distribution of antiâ€‘draft leaflets under the Espionage Act violate the **First Amendment (applied to the federal government)**?
>     
> 2. What doctrinal test governs when political speech may be criminalized in wartime?
>     

> **Holding / Vote Map**  
> â€“â€¯**Unanimous 9â€‘0** (Opinion: **Holmes,â€¯J.**; joined by Whiteâ€¯C.J., McKenna, Day, Vanâ€¯Devanter, Pitney, McReynolds, Brandeis, Clarke).  
> â€“â€¯**Convictions affirmed**; Act constitutional as applied.

> **Majority Reasoning (Justiceâ€¯Holmes)**  
> **Stepâ€¯1â€¯â€“â€¯Contextual Lens**  
> â€“â€¯_â€œThe character of every act depends upon the circumstances in which it is done.â€_ Wartime context amplifies the governmentâ€™s interest.  
> **Stepâ€¯2â€¯â€“â€¯Birth of the _Clearâ€‘andâ€‘Presentâ€‘Danger_ Test**  
> â€“â€¯Speech is unprotected when its **â€œwords are used in such circumstances and are of such a nature as to create a clear and present danger that they will bring about ==the substantive evils Congress has a right to prevent.==â€**  
> â€“â€¯Analogy: _falsely shouting fire in a crowded theatre_â€”speechâ€™s proximity & likelihood of harm govern.  
> **Stepâ€¯3â€¯â€“â€¯Application**  
> â€“â€¯Leafletâ€™s explicit call to **oppose the draft** during active mobilization posed a danger both ==**â€œclearâ€ (imminent) and â€œpresentâ€ (proximate)**.==  
> **Stepâ€¯4â€¯â€“â€¯First Amendment Scope**  
> ==â€“â€¯First Amendment is not absolute; it does not protect speech that **undermines lawful government functions in wartime.**==

> **Scrutiny Level**  
> â€“â€¯Not yet â€œstrict scrutinyâ€; rather an **adâ€‘hoc balancing** strongly deferential to wartime legislative judgment.

> **Significance / Legacy**  
> â€“â€¯First articulation of the **Clearâ€‘andâ€‘Presentâ€‘Danger test**, soon reâ€‘examined in _Abrams_ (Holmes dissent) and refined in _Whitney_ (Brandeis concurrence).  
> â€“â€¯Illustrates early **Holmesâ€‘Brandeis civilâ€‘libertarian evolution**: unanimous here, but their views liberalize within a single year.  
> â€“â€¯Framework survives through the 1920sâ€“40s until supplanted by **â€œincitement to imminent lawless actionâ€** in _Brandenburg v. Ohio_ (1969).  
> â€“â€¯Stands as canonical example of **wartime speech restrictions** and the tension between security and dissent.

> **Socraticâ€‘Class Landmines & Answers**  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ If the leaflets had advocated only **repeal** of the draft but not **resistance**, same result?  
> â†’ Probably not; Holmes emphasizes the _practical obstruction_ language. Pure policy advocacy would be protected.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ How â€œclearâ€ is â€œclearâ€â€”does a single leaflet suffice?  
> â†’ Holmes never quantifies; later cases (_Debs_, _Abrams_, _Gitlow_) show Court sliding toward abstract ideological punishments until _Brandenburg_.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Where does incorporation stand?  
> â†’ _Schenck_ binds only the **federal** government; states were unrestricted until _Gitlow_ (1925) began selective incorporation.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Could Congress criminalize **possession** of antiâ€‘draft literature?  
> â†’ Under _Schenck_, perhaps yes during war, but modern doctrine demands **advocacy + imminence**.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Does â€œfireâ€‘inâ€‘aâ€‘theatreâ€ still govern?  
> â†’ Rhetorically famous, doctrinally outdated; modern incitement test is _Brandenburg_â€™s **intent + imminence + likelihood** triad.

### 2. _Dennisâ€¯v.â€¯Unitedâ€¯States_,â€¯341â€¯U.S.â€¯494â€¯(1951)

> **Factsâ€¯/â€¯Proceduralâ€¯Posture**  
> â€“â€¯1948â€‘49: **Eugeneâ€¯Dennis** (General Secretary, Communist Party USA) and 10 other party leaders indicted in the **S.D.N.Y.** for **conspiracy to violate Â§â€¯2(a)(1) of the Smith Act** (advocating the violent overthrow of the U.S. government).  
> â€“â€¯Lengthy nineâ€‘month jury trial before Judge **Harold Medina**; prosecution rested largely on FBI informants & Marxistâ€‘Leninist texts. All defendants convicted; received 3â€“5â€‘year sentences.  
> â€“â€¯**Second Circuit (Hand,â€¯J.)** affirms, adopting the famous ==â€œgravity of the evil, discounted by its improbabilityâ€== formulation.  
> â€“â€¯SCOTUS argument Dec.â€¯4â€‘7â€¯1950; decision Juneâ€¯4â€¯1951, at the height of Korean War & McCarthy hearings.

> **Governmentâ€™s Theory (Attorney General J.â€¯Howard McGrath)**  
> â€“â€¯**Communist Party is a disciplined conspiratorial apparatus** seeking to foment revolution when geopolitically opportune; leadershipâ€™s teachings pose a **latent but grave threat**.  
> â€“â€¯No Firstâ€‘Amendment immunity for a **conspiracy** to advocate illegal violence; Congress validly acted to **neutralize subversive organizations** before they strike.  
> â€“â€¯Courts must weigh **national survival**; cannot wait for the â€œcertainly impendingâ€ spark.

> **Defendantsâ€™ Theory (Dennis etâ€¯al.)**  
> â€“â€¯Indictment punishes **speech and belief, not action**; teaching Marxism is protected advocacy of political theory.  
> â€“â€¯Smith Act overbroad & vague; violates **clearâ€andâ€‘presentâ€‘danger** standard of _Schneider_, _Thomas v. Collins_, _Bridges_.  
> â€“â€¯First Amendment demands proof of **imminent incitement**; prosecution showed only academic discussion and party texts.

> **Issues**
> 1. Is the **Smith Actâ€™s prohibition of conspiracy to advocate violent overthrow** facially or asâ€‘applied unconstitutional under the First Amendment?
> 2. What is the proper test for â€œdangerâ€ posed by **doctrinal advocacy** in the Coldâ€‘War context?

> **Holdingsâ€¯/â€¯Vote Map**  
> â€“â€¯**Plurality 4â€‘1â€‘4 (Overall 6â€‘2 to affirm)**
> - **Vinsonâ€¯C.J.** opinion (joined by Reed, Burton, Minton).
> - **Frankfurter** concurrence (joined by Jackson): same result, separate balancing.
> - **Jackson** solo concurrence (sharper focus on â€œconspiracyâ€).
> - **Black & Douglas** dissents (separate; sweeping speech protections).
> - **Clark** recused. â‡’ **Convictions affirmed; Smith Act upheld.**

> **Plurality Reasoning (Chiefâ€¯Justiceâ€¯Vinson, joined by Reed, Burton, Minton)**  
> **Stepâ€¯1 â€“ Adopt Judge Handâ€™s Balancing**  
> â€“â€¯Court embraces the **â€œgravityâ€‘ofâ€‘evil discounted by its improbabilityâ€** test: ==if **evil is great enough,** government need not await nearâ€‘certainty.==
> **Stepâ€¯2 â€“ Distinguish Abstract Doctrine vs. Active Conspiracy**  
> â€“â€¯Defendants are ==**not mere philosophers**;== they lead a hierarchical organization committed to Leninist discipline.
> â€“â€¯_â€œ==Advocacyâ€_ under Smith Act means **organizing for action**,== not casual discussion.  
> **Stepâ€¯3 â€“ Judicial Role in National Security**  
> â€“â€¯Courts must acknowledge **Congressional/executive intelligence** on Communist tactics; deferential but not rubberâ€‘stamp.  
> **Result:** Present danger **adequate**; First Amendment yields to collective security.

> **Frankfurter Concurrence (joined by Jackson)**  
> â€“â€¯Advocates explicit **balancing test**: courts must weigh **societyâ€™s right to selfâ€‘preservation** against speech.  
> â€“â€¯Warns against â€œabsolutismâ€ of First Amendment; endorses **legislative factâ€‘finding** on subversive threats.

> **Jackson Concurrence (solo)**  
> â€“â€¯Frames defendants as ==akin to **enemy agents**;== stresses **conspiracy** as actionable independent of overt incitement.

> **Dissent (Justiceâ€¯Black)**  
> â€“â€¯First Amendment is **absolute**: Congress may punish only **direct incitement or action**, not teaching.  
> â€“â€¯Balancing â€œgravityâ€ vs. â€œprobabilityâ€ hands judges a **censorâ€™s veto**.

> **Dissent (Justiceâ€¯Douglas)**  
> â€“â€¯Danger here is **too remote**; free society must risk â€œmisguided orthodoxyâ€ to preserve liberty. 
> â€“â€¯Smith Act collapses distinction between **doctrine and advocacy**; cites Jeffersonâ€™s â€œerror of opinion may be tolerated.â€

> **Level of Scrutiny**  
> â€“â€¯Effectively a **Handâ€‘style adâ€‘hoc balancing** â‰ˆ intermediate scrutiny weighted by national security; far from modern _Brandenburg_ strictness.

> **Significance / Fallout**  
> â€“â€¯**Highâ€‘water mark of Coldâ€‘War repression**; legitimated broad antiâ€‘Communist prosecutions.  
> â€“â€¯Soon retrenched: **_Yates v. U.S._ (1957)** narrows â€œadvocacy,â€ insisting on **â€œurge to action.â€**  
> â€“â€¯Overruled in effect by **_Brandenburg v. Ohio_ (1969)** (imminentâ€‘lawlessâ€‘action test).  
> â€“â€¯Exposed fissures in Firstâ€‘Amendment theory: **absolutism vs. balancing**; seeded modern doctrine that **abstract advocacy is protected**.  
> â€“â€¯Political echo: fueled McCarthyism yet also provoked intellectual backlash leading to cases like **_Keyishian_** (1967) and **_Braynen_**.

> **Socraticâ€‘Class Landmines & Answers**  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Are _Dennis_ and _Schenck_ formally overruled?  
> â†’ _Dennis_â€™s test is explicitly rejected by _Brandenburg_; _Schenck_â€™s result survives only where speech meets **incitement + imminence + likelihood**.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Why didnâ€™t the Court require **proof of an overt act**?  
> â†’ Conspiracy law criminalizes **agreement plus intent**; plurality viewed Party structure itself as **â€œclear and presentâ€**.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Could the same prosecution succeed today?  
> â†’ Unlikely: _Brandenburg_ demands **specific intent to produce imminent lawless action**; abstract Marxist seminars are protected.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ What doctrinal thread links _Schenck_, _Dennis_, _Keyishian_, _Brandenburg_?  
> â†’ An arc from **wartime deference** â†’ **Cold War balancing** â†’ **academicâ€‘freedom recognition** â†’ **robust incitement standard** safeguarding ideological speech.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Does _Dennis_ permit suppression of **ISIS online propaganda**?  
> â†’ Only if government shows **direct incitement of imminent terrorist acts**; modern courts apply _Holder v. Humanitarian Law Project_ (2010) and _Brandenburg_.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ Is _Handâ€™s formula_ dead?  
> â†’ As Firstâ€‘Amendment doctrine, yes; but echoes survive in **nationalâ€‘security FISA warrants** and **materialâ€‘support statutes**, where courts still weigh **severity vs. likelihood** under a rationalâ€‘basisâ€‘plus lens.

Both cases illuminate the **dialectic between national exigency and constitutional liberty**:

|Era|Perceived Threat|Doctrinal Test|Result|Later Treatment|
|---|---|---|---|---|
|**1919â€¯â€“â€¯_Schenck_**|German sabotage, wartime morale|_Clear & Present Danger_ (nascent)|Speech punished|Softâ€‘limited by evolution toward imminence|
|**1951â€¯â€“â€¯_Dennis_**|Global Communist conspiracy|Handâ€™s **Gravityâ€¯Ã·â€¯Probability** balancing|Speech punished|_Yates_ narrows; _Brandenburg_ supplants|

### 3. _Brandenburgâ€¯v.â€¯Ohio_,â€¯395â€¯U.S.â€¯444â€¯(1969)

> **Factsâ€¯/â€¯Proceduralâ€¯Posture**  
> â€“â€¯**Clarenceâ€¯Brandenburg**, a Klan â€œImperial Wizardâ€ from rural Ohio, invited a Cincinnati TV reporter to film a ==**crossâ€‘burning rally**== on a farm in Hamilton County (Juneâ€¯1964).  
> â€“â€¯The broadcast showed hooded speakers railing ==against Blacks and Jews== and warning that if the government continued to â€œsuppress the white Caucasian race,â€ there might have to be â€œrevengeanceâ€ against Congress, the President, and the Court.  
> â€“â€¯Brandenburg was charged under **Ohio Rev. Code Â§â€¯2923.13 (Criminal Syndicalism)**, which outlawed advocating â€œcrime, sabotage, violence, or unlawful methods of terrorismâ€ to effect political change and assembling with any group formed to teach or advocate such doctrine.  
> â€“â€¯**County Court** convicted; sentence: $1,000 fine plus 1â€“10â€¯years. Ohio Court of Appeals affirmed; **Ohio Supreme Court dismissed** without opinion. SCOTUS noted probable jurisdiction; argument Feb.â€¯27â€¯1969; decision Juneâ€¯9â€¯1969.

> **Stateâ€™s Theory (Prosecutor & Ohio AG)**  
> â€“â€¯The statute mirrors **Whitney** and **Dennis**; it punishes **advocacy, not mere abstract doctrine**, of violent overthrow.  
> â€“â€¯Brandenburgâ€™s own words and affiliation with the Klan create a sufficient **danger to public order**.

> **Petitionerâ€™s Theory (Brandenburg)**  
> â€“â€¯Speech was **political hyperbole** with no call to _immediate_ violence; statute punishes mere abstract advocacy, violating the **First & Fourteenth Amendments**.  
> â€“â€¯**Yates** and **Noto** require proof of _incitement to action_, not abstract teaching.

> **Issues**
> 1. Does Ohioâ€™s criminalâ€‘syndicalism law violate the First and Fourteenth Amendments by punishing advocacy of violence irrespective of imminence or likelihood?
> 2. What is the constitutional test for distinguishing protected advocacy from unprotected incitement?

> **Holdingâ€¯/â€¯Vote Map**  
> â€“â€¯**Perâ€¯Curiam, 8â€Šâ€“â€Š0.** (Justice Fortas had just resigned.)  
> â€“â€¯Conviction **reversed**; **statute unconstitutional ==on its face**.==  
> â€“â€¯**Black & Douglas** each filed short concurrences emphasizing absolutism.

> **Core Reasoning (Perâ€¯Curiam)**  
> **Stepâ€¯1 â€“ Jettison the â€œBalancingâ€ of _Dennis_**  
> â€“â€¯Reâ€‘examines _Whitney_ and _Dennis_; rules that **mere advocacy of abstract doctrine is not enough**.  
> **Stepâ€¯2 â€“ Announce the _Brandenburg_ Incitement Standard**  
> â€“â€¯*â€œThe constitutional guarantees of free speech and free press do not permit a State to forbid or proscribe advocacy of the use of force or of law violation ==**except** where such advocacy is directed to inciting or producing imminent lawless action and is likely to incite or produce such action.â€==
> â€“â€¯Two prongs: **(i) Intent/Direction** and **(ii) Imminence/Likelihood.**  
> **Stepâ€¯3 â€“ Apply to Facts**  
> â€“â€¯Brandenburgâ€™s statements, although racist and violent in tone, were **conditional and futureâ€‘oriented**; ==no imminent mob, no concrete plan, thus protected.==  
> **Stepâ€¯4 â€“ Overrule Sub Silentio**  
> â€“â€¯Explicitly **overrules _Whitney v. California_ (1927)**; implicitly guts _Dennis_ balancing.

> **Concurrences**  
> â€“â€¯**Black**: First Amendment bars any punishment for mere advocacy; ==rejects all â€œclearâ€‘andâ€‘presentâ€‘dangerâ€ formulations.==  
> â€“â€¯**Douglas**: Balancing speech against security invites censorship; only ==**â€œhotâ€ incitement== may be punished.

> **Level of Scrutiny / Doctrine Articulated**  
> â€“â€¯Establishes the modern **â€œImminentâ€‘Lawlessâ€‘Actionâ€ test**â€”effectively **strict scrutiny plus temporal and probabilistic filters**.

> **Significance / Fallout**  
> â€“â€¯Canonical pivot from **handâ€‘waving balancing** to a **brightâ€‘line test** that protects even extremist advocacy.  
> â€“â€¯Framework now governs everything from **terrorâ€‘recruitment cases** to **socialâ€‘media exhortations**.  
> â€“â€¯Influential progeny: _Hess_ (1973), _NAACP v. Claiborne Hardware_ (1982), _Bible Believers_ (6thâ€¯Cir.â€¯2015).  
> â€“â€¯Cited to show that **â€œhate speechâ€ is protected unless it meets the _Brandenburg_ standard**â€”fueling campusâ€‘speech debates and onlineâ€‘platform policy.

> **Socraticâ€‘Class Landmines & Answers**  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œDoes _Brandenburg_ protect direct instructions to build bombs posted online?â€  
> â†’ Only if there is **no intent or likelihood of immediate use**; otherwise, _Holder v. HLP_ & **materialâ€‘support statutes** apply.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œHow â€˜imminentâ€™ is â€˜imminentâ€™?â€  
> â†’ Seconds or minutes, not days; **abstraction â‰  imminence** (see _Hess_: â€˜Weâ€™ll take the streets **later**â€™ is protected).  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œDoes the decision rely on viewpoint neutrality?â€  
> â†’ Yes. Statute failed to distinguish **abstract doctrine vs. incitement** and thus was viewpointâ€‘based and overbroad.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œWhat if Brandenburg had handed out weapons at the rally?â€  
> â†’ Act plus intent â†’ **unprotected incitement + aidingâ€‘andâ€‘abetting**; criminal liability stands.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œIs _Brandenburg_ absolute?â€  
> â†’ No; **â€œtrue threats,â€ â€œfighting words,â€ and speech integral to crime** remain outside protection.

### 4. _Chaplinskyâ€¯v.â€¯Newâ€¯Hampshire_,â€¯315â€¯U.S.â€¯568â€¯(1942)

> **Factsâ€¯/â€¯Proceduralâ€¯Posture**  
> â€“â€¯**Walterâ€¯Chaplinsky**, a Jehovahâ€™s Witness, was distributing literature in downtown Rochester, N.H. Crowds grew hostile; police led him to a patrolman.  
> â€“â€¯Chaplinsky shouted: ==**â€œYou are a Godâ€‘damned racketeerâ€** and **â€œa damned Fascist.â€**==  
> â€“â€¯Arrested under **N.H. Rev. Laws ch. 378, Â§â€¯2**: criminalizes addressing â€œany offensive, derisive or annoying wordâ€ to another person in a public place.  
> â€“â€¯Convicted in **Municipal Court**; $50 fine. New Hampshire Supreme Court affirmed, holding the statute limited to words that tend to cause a breach of the peace. SCOTUS granted cert.; argument Feb.â€¯5â€¯1942; decision Marchâ€¯9â€¯1942.

> **Stateâ€™s Theory (County Attorney & N.H. AG)**  
> â€“â€¯Statute targets **â€œfighting wordsâ€**â€”epithets likely to provoke the average person to retaliate; narrow focus on **immediate breaches of peace.**  
> â€“â€¯Faces of statute construed by state courts to avoid vagueness.

> **Petitionerâ€™s Theory (Chaplinsky)**  
> â€“â€¯Statute is **overbroad and vague**; criminalizes protected expression including religious or political criticism.  
> â€“â€¯Words spoken were **political opinion** insulated by the First Amendment (as incorporated via Fourteenth).

> **Issues**
> 1. Does a state statute punishing â€œoffensive, derisive, or annoyingâ€ words violate the **First and Fourteenth Amendments**?
> 2. Are there **categorical classes of speech** outside Firstâ€‘Amendment protection?

> **Holding / Vote Map**  
> â€“â€¯**Unanimous 9â€Šâ€“â€Š0** (Opinion: **Murphy,â€¯J.**). Conviction **affirmed**.

> **Majority Reasoning (Justiceâ€¯Murphy)**  
> **Stepâ€¯1 â€“ Twoâ€‘Tier Theory of Speech**  
> â€“â€¯Some utterances ==â€œare **no essential part of any exposition of ideas** and are of such slight social value that any benefit is clearly outweighed by the social interest in order and morality.â€== 
> â€“â€¯Enumerates **unprotected categories**: ==obscenity, profanity, libel, and **â€œfighting words.â€**==  
> **Stepâ€¯2 â€“ Define Fighting Words**  
> â€“â€¯â€œ**Words which by their very utterance inflict injury or tend to incite an immediate breach of the peace.**â€
> â€“â€¯Objective ==â€œaverage manâ€ standard; context matters.==  
> **Stepâ€¯3 â€“ Apply to Chaplinsky**  
> â€“â€¯â€œGodâ€‘damned racketeerâ€ / â€œdamned Fascistâ€ directed faceâ€‘toâ€‘face at an officer = classic fighting words; statute, as narrowed, punishes only that subset, so **no overbreadth or vagueness.**

> **Level of Scrutiny**  
> â€“â€¯No balancing; **categorical exclusion**: fighting words are **unprotected**, so ordinary rationalâ€‘basis review of statute.

> **Significance / Subsequent Doctrine**  
> â€“â€¯Landmark creation of the **Fightingâ€‘Words Doctrine**.  
> â€“â€¯Later cases **narrow the category**:
> - **Cohen v. California** (1971) â€“ â€œF*** the Draftâ€ jacket **protected** (not a faceâ€‘toâ€‘face insult).
> - **Gooding v. Wilson** (1972), **Lewis v. New Orleans** (1974) â€“ statutes voided for overbreadth.
> - **R.A.V. v. St. Paul** (1992) â€“ Viewpointâ€‘based bans on fighting words struck down.  
>     â€“â€¯Modern courts seldom uphold convictions on pure fightingâ€‘words grounds; doctrine survives mainly in **assaultive faceâ€‘toâ€‘face insults**.

> **Socraticâ€‘Class Landmines & Answers**  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œWhy is â€˜F*** the Draftâ€™ protected but â€˜Youâ€™re a damned Fascistâ€™ not?â€  
> â†’ **Audience & focus**: Cohenâ€™s message was **political, general, nonâ€‘confrontational**; Chaplinskyâ€™s was a **direct personal epithet** likely to trigger violence.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œDoes Chaplinsky survive _R.A.V._?â€  
> â†’ The _category_ survives; but regulations must be **contentâ€‘ and viewpointâ€‘neutral**â€”no targeting specific ideologies.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œDifference between fighting words and â€˜true threatsâ€™?â€  
> â†’ _True threat_: speaker **intends** to instill fear of bodily harm; _fighting words_: likely to **provoke target to violence**.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œIf Chaplinsky had shouted the same words online?â€  
> â†’ Likely **protected**; absence of immediate physical confrontation undermines the fightingâ€‘words rationale.  
> â€“â€¯_Q:_ â€œCan states criminalize hate slurs as fighting words?â€  
> â†’ Only if the statute is **neutral** (covers all epithets) and speech is **faceâ€‘toâ€‘face**, **likely to provoke** violence; otherwise _R.A.V._ and _Virginia v. Black_ constraints apply.

#### Quick Comparative Table

| Case            | Year | Unprotected Category       | Doctrinal Test Announced                                                                         | Result                                | Modern Status                                  |
| --------------- | ---- | -------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------- |
| **Chaplinsky**  | 1942 | **Fighting Words**         | Categorical exclusion; words that by utterance inflict injury / provoke immediate breach         | Conviction **affirmed**               | Survives but **narrowed** by _Cohen_, _R.A.V._ |
| **Brandenburg** | 1969 | **Incitement to Violence** | Speech unprotected **only** if _directed to_ and _likely to_ produce **imminent lawless action** | Conviction **reversed**; statute void | Governs modern incitement / extremist speech   |

### Additional Context

#### ðŸ”¹ First Amendment Doctrinal Categories â€“ **Exceptions to Protection**

| **Category**                     | **Standard / Case**                             | **When Speech Is Unprotected**                                                                     | **Typical Application Contexts**                                         |
| -------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| **Fighting Words**               | _Chaplinsky v. N.H._ (1942)                     | **Face-to-face, personal insults** likely to provoke **immediate retaliatory violence**            | Street confrontations, protests, bar fights (rarely upheld today)        |
| **True Threats**                 | _Virginia v. Black_ (2003), _Counterman_ (2023) | A **serious expression of intent to commit violence**, made with knowledge or recklessness         | Threats to President, stalking, online threats, violent domestic abuse   |
| **Incitement to Lawless Action** | _Brandenburg v. Ohio_ (1969)                    | Speech **directed to inciting** imminent lawless conduct and **likely to produce** it              | Extremist rallies, mob agitation, terror propaganda                      |
| **Harassment (Work/School)**     | _Meritor Savings v. Vinson_ (1986), Title IX    | **Severe, pervasive, and objectively offensive conduct** interfering with education/employment     | Sexual, racial, gender-based verbal conduct in **schools or workplaces** |
| **Defamation (Libel/Slander)**   | _New York Times v. Sullivan_ (1964)             | **False factual assertions** causing reputational harm; public figures must show **actual malice** | Press, social media, political campaigns, personal attacks               |
| **Obscenity**                    | _Miller v. California_ (1973)                   | Appeals to **prurient interest**, depicts sexual conduct **patently offensive**, lacks value       | Pornography prosecutions (mostly obsolete with Internet age)             |
| **Child Pornography**            | _New York v. Ferber_ (1982)                     | Any depiction of minors in sexual conduct; **no value test needed**                                | Possession, production, or distribution involving real children          |
| **Commercial Speech (Limited)**  | _Central Hudson_ (1980)                         | May be regulated if misleading, unlawful, or the regulation meets **intermediate scrutiny**        | Ads for illegal goods, misleading health claims                          |

#### ðŸ”¹ Key Distinctions Between Categories

| **Doctrine**             | **Whatâ€™s Being Provoked**                                                       | **Core Test**                                                         | **Protected Ifâ€¦**                          |
| ------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------ |
| **Fighting Words**       | **Violence from the listener _toward speaker_**                                 | Face-to-face insult **likely to provoke retaliation**                 | General vulgarity not directed at a person |
| **Incitement (Provoke)** | **Unlawful action _by the listener_** -- provoking violence against the speaker | Intent + imminence + likelihood (_Brandenburg_)                       | No immediate danger, abstract ideology     |
| **True Threat**          | **Fear or terror _in the target_**                                              | Serious intent to harm + subjective awareness (_Black_, _Counterman_) | Conditional or hyperbolic political speech |
| **Harassment**           | **Systematic abuse _within institutions_**                                      | Severe, pervasive, objectively offensive                              | Single or offhand comment in public        |
| **Defamation**           | **Reputational injury through **false** speech                                  | False statement of fact + damage + culpability (_Sullivan_)           | Opinion, parody, hyperbole are protected   |

#### ðŸŸ¢ Application Framework â€“ _Watts v. U.S._ (1969) Factors for â€œTrue Threatsâ€

|**Watts Factors**|**Explanation**|
|---|---|
|**Context of Speech**|Political rally vs. private threat vs. online comment|
|**Audience Reaction**|Was it laughed off, ignored, or taken seriously?|
|**Conditional or Not**|â€œIf they draft me, thenâ€¦â€ â†’ suggests **lack of immediacy or seriousness**|

> âœ… _Watts_: â€œIf they ever make me carry a rifle, the first man I want to get in my sights is LBJ.â€  
> ðŸ”Ž Context = political protest; Audience = laughed; Threat = conditional â†’ **Protected hyperbole**.

#### ðŸŸ¢ Key Case: _R.A.V. v. St. Paul_ (1992) â€” Viewpoint Discrimination within Unprotected Categories

- **Held**: Even within an unprotected category (e.g., fighting words), the **government cannot engage in content- or viewpoint-based discrimination**.

| **R.A.V. Principle**                                                                                                                                                                                |
| --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| A regulation **cannot criminalize only certain views** within fighting words or threats (e.g., only racist epithets) unless it targets a **neutral, proscribable basis (e.g., threats to safety)**. |

> ðŸ” E.g., You can't criminalize **cross-burning with racist motivation** but allow it for other motives, unless you're regulating **true threats** (_Virginia v. Black_ carved out this nuance).

#### ðŸŸ¢ **Key Case:â€¯_Virginiaâ€¯v.â€¯Black_â€¯(2003)â€¯â€” â€œTrueâ€‘Threatâ€ Definition and Permissible Regulation**

| **_Virginia v.â€¯Black_ Principle**                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       |
| ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| The State may punish speech as a **true threat** only when the speaker <br>**(1)** _directs_ the statement toward a specific individual or clearly identifiable group **and** <br>**(2)** _conveys_ a serious expression of intent to commit unlawful violence **and** <br>**(3)** _acts with the purpose (or conscious awareness) of placing the target in fear_ of bodily harm or death. The speaker need **not** actually intend to follow through; the constitutional interest is in shielding the target from fear, the disruption that fear causes, and the possibility that the threatened violence might occur. |

#### ðŸ”¹ Example Applications: Your Hypotheticals

| Statement                                     | Fighting Words?                      | True Threat? | Incitement?   | Protected? | Why?                                                             |
| --------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------ | ------------ | ------------- | ---------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------- |
| â€œYou are fucking ugly.â€                       | Maybe (face-to-face)                 | âŒ            | âŒ             | âœ…          | Crude insult; not likely to provoke violence                     |
| â€œFuck you, you little piece of shit.â€         | Possibly (if hostile + face-to-face) | âŒ            | âŒ             | âœ…/âŒ        | Maybe punishable as fighting words, but extremely rare           |
| â€œIâ€™ll shoot you in your ugly face.â€           | âŒ                                    | âœ…            | âŒ             | âŒ          | Likely unprotected true threat (intent + fear)                   |
| â€œWe should blow up Congress.â€                 | âŒ                                    | âŒ            | âœ… if imminent | Maybe      | Depends on **intent + likelihood** of action                     |
| â€œLetâ€™s kill all women tomorrow.â€ (online)     | âŒ                                    | âœ…/Maybe      | âœ…/Maybe       | âŒ/âœ…        | If directed + imminent = incitement/threat; abstract = protected |
| â€œDeny. Delay. Depose. You guys are the next!â€ |                                      |              |               |            |                                                                  |

## Week 7.1 -- Obscenity

### 1. **_Millerâ€¯v.â€¯California_,â€¯413â€¯U.S.â€¯15â€¯(1973)**

â€” _Burger Court redraws the map of â€œobscenity,â€ coining the threeâ€‘part **Miller test**_

**Factsâ€¯&â€¯Procedural Posture**
- 1969â€‘70â€¯â†’â€¯**Marvin Miller** runs a directâ€‘mail â€œadultâ€ business in Southernâ€¯California.
- Five catalog brochuresâ€”packed with graphic photos entitled _Intercourse_, _Manâ€‘Woman_, _Sex Orgies Illustrated_, _An Illustrated History of Pornography_, plus the film _Marital Intercourse_â€”are mailed unsolicited to a Newportâ€¯Beach restaurant; the managerâ€™s mother also sees them and calls police.
- Charged under **Cal.â€¯Pen.â€¯Code Â§â€¯311.2(a)** (knowingly distributing obscene matter).
- **Orangeâ€¯County jury** convicts; Superior Court imposes a fine + prison term (sentence stayed).
- Appellateâ€¯Dept., Superiorâ€¯Court affirms without opinion; California Supreme Court denies review.
- **SCOTUS** notes probable jurisdiction (Janâ€¯1972), argues Novâ€¯7â€¯1972; reargued Janâ€¯18â€¯1973; decision **Juneâ€¯21â€¯1973**.

**Stateâ€™s Theory**
- Brochures graphically depict â€œhardâ€‘coreâ€ sexual acts; no artistic or literary merit; statute tracks _Roth_ and _Memoirs_ standards; California has a compelling interest in shielding unwilling recipients.

**Millerâ€™s Theory**
- Mailings protected by the First Amendment; existing â€œutterly without redeeming social valueâ€ standard (from _Memoirs_) not satisfied; statute fails to give fair notice or require national standards â†’ unconstitutional vagueness/overbreadth.

**Issues Presented**
1. What constitutional test should govern state regulation of obscenity after _Roth_, _Memoirs_, and _Stanley_?
2. Does Californiaâ€™s conviction violate the First and Fourteenth Amendments?

**Holding & Vote**
- **5â€¯â€“â€¯4** (Burger, White, Blackmun, Powell, Rehnquistâ€¯â‡¢â€¯majority; Douglas, Brennan, Stewart, Marshallâ€¯â‡¢â€¯dissent).
- Conviction **vacated and remanded** (because jury applied the wrong, tooâ€‘lenient â€œsocialâ€‘valueâ€ test); **California statute survives** so long as it incorporates the new standard.

**Core Reasoning (Burger, C.J.)**
1. **Rejection of â€œSocialâ€‘Valueâ€ & Nationalâ€‘Standard formulas**
    - The _Memoirs_ rule (â€œutterly without redeeming social valueâ€) proved unworkable; jury confusion + deluge of appeals.
2. **Announcement of the **Miller Test****
    > â€œThe First Amendment allows regulation _only_ of works that, **taken as a whole**:  
    > â€¯**(a)** appeal to the _prurient interest_ of an **average person applying contemporary _community_ standards**;  
    > â€¯**(b)** depict or describe **patently offensive** sexual conduct _specifically defined by state law_; and
    > â€¯**(c)** **lack serious Literary, Artistic, Political, or Scientific (SLAPS) value**.â€
3. **Community Variability**
    - National uniformity is impossible; what Maine must tolerate need not equal Lasâ€¯Vegas.
4. **Fair Notice & Enumerated Conduct**
    - States must spell out in the statuteâ€”or authoritative judicial constructionâ€”the sexual acts subject to prohibition.
5. **Application**
    - California courts used an outdated test; case remanded for reassessment under the new standard.

**Brennanâ€™s Dissent (joined by Stewart & Marshall)**
- Concedes personal exhaustion with obscenity lineâ€‘drawing; now favors **full Firstâ€‘Amendment protection** for adult pornography absent direct assault on juveniles or unwilling viewers.
- Predicts continued â€œinstitutional stressâ€ and chilling effect.

**==Douglas Dissent==**
- â€œObscenityâ€ never appears in the Constitution; censorship must comeâ€”if at allâ€”via amendment, not judicial invention.

**Doctrinal Takeaways**
- **SLAPS** replaces â€œutterly withoutâ€ test.
- **Community standards = local**, not national, but SLAPS value judged by a **reasonable national jury**, preserving some objectivity.
- Crime requires **depiction of sexual conduct**, not mere profanity/nudity.
- Jury still factâ€‘finder; judges supply limiting instructions.

**Impact & Legacy**
- Obscenity prosecutions revived (e.g., â€œDeepâ€¯Throat,â€ 1976).
- **â€œMiller testâ€** governs U.S. obscenity law to present day; survives technological shifts (VHS, internet).
- Online era: courts wrestle with which â€œcommunityâ€ applies in cyberspace; some circuits adopt an â€œinterested communityâ€ approach, others national for prongâ€¯(a).
- Contentâ€‘platform policies (YouTube, OnlyFans) silently embed Miller criteria.

**Socratic Traps & Answers**
- _Q: Do community standards vary within a state?_ â†’ Yes; jury reflects its jurisdictionâ€™s norms.
- _Q: Does SLAPS value look to local views?_ â†’ No; objective, _reasonableâ€‘person_ perspective.
- _Q: What about violent but nonâ€‘sexual gore?_ â†’ Not obscenity; analyze under general indecency/harm provisions.
- _Q: Is child pornography covered?_ â†’ No; separate categorical exclusion _Newâ€¯Yorkâ€¯v.â€¯Ferber_ (1982).
- _Q: Can â€œmere nudityâ€ be obscene?_ â†’ Rarely; prurient + patently offensive + SLAPSâ€‘less threshold rarely met by simple nudity.

**Mnemonic**

> **â€œPâ€‘Pâ€‘S / Sâ€‘Lâ€‘Aâ€‘Pâ€‘Sâ€** â†’ _Prurient, Patently offensive, Specific conduct & Lacks Serious Literaryâ€‘Artisticâ€‘Politicalâ€‘Scientific value_.

**Bottom Line**  
Miller institutionalized a threeâ€‘pronged **localâ€‘standards + SLAPS** framework: only the most graphic, valueless sexual depictions aimed at arousal fall outside Firstâ€‘Amendment protection; mere offensiveness or nudity does not suffice.

### 2. **_Cohenâ€¯v.â€¯California_,â€¯403â€¯U.S.â€¯15â€¯(1971)**

â€” _The Courtâ€™s ode to the emotive power of fourâ€‘letter words_

**Factsâ€¯&â€¯Procedural Posture**
- **Aprilâ€¯26â€¯1968**â€¯â†’â€¯**Paul Robert Cohen**, 19â€‘yearâ€‘old Vietnamâ€‘war critic, enters the **Losâ€¯Angeles County Courthouse** corridor wearing a jacket handâ€‘painted **â€œF**kâ€¯theâ€¯Draft.â€**
- No chanting, no leaflets, no threats; women and children present.
- Arrested under **Cal.â€¯Pen.â€¯Code Â§â€¯415**: â€œmaliciously and willfully disturb the peace â€¦ by offensive conduct.â€
- **Municipal Court** convicts; sentence: 30â€¯days in jail.
- **California Court of Appeal** affirms, defining jacket as â€œoffensive conduct.â€ State Supreme Court denies review.
- **SCOTUS** grants certiorari (Octâ€¯1969), hears argument Febâ€¯22â€¯1971; decision **Juneâ€¯7â€¯1971**.

**Californiaâ€™s Position**
- The jacketâ€™s language is **likely to provoke violence** (â€œfighting wordsâ€) or at least shocks captive observers; courthouse demands decorum; statute valid.

**Cohenâ€™s Position**
- Pure political expression; word is vulgar but **not** obscene or directed at anyone; passersâ€‘by can avert eyes; statute void for vagueness & overbreadth.

**Issues**
1. Does punishing display of a single profane word on a jacket violate freeâ€‘speech guarantees?
2. Are â€œoffensiveâ€ words outside Firstâ€‘Amendment protection absent obscenity, threats, or incitement?

**Holding & Vote**
- **6â€¯â€“â€¯3** (Harlanâ€¯II writes; joined by Douglas, Brennan, Stewart, White, Marshall).
- Conviction **reversed**; Â§â€¯415 unconstitutional ==as applied.==
- **Dissent (Blackmun, joined by Burger & Black)**: jacket â‰ˆ conduct; falls within _Chaplinsky_ â€œfighting words.â€

**Majority Reasoning (Harlan)**
1. **This is Speech, Not Conduct** â€“ Only the communicative content was punished; no disruptive actions.
2. **Not Obscenity, Not Fighting Words** â€“ â€œFâ€‘â€‘â€‘â€ lacks erotic detail (**Roth**), and message not aimed at any particular listener (**Chaplinsky**).
3. **No Captiveâ€‘Audience Problem** â€“ Brief exposure in a public corridor; people can simply **â€œavert their eyes.â€** -- Not fighting words
4. **Emotive & Cognitive Dimensions of Speech** â€“ The Constitution guards not just ideas but the **intensity** with which they are expressed; one manâ€™s vulgarity is anotherâ€™s lyric.
5. **Slipperyâ€‘Slope / Boundless Censorâ€™s Veto** â€“ If this word is banned, where stop? Government cannot sanitize public debate to placate the squeamish.
6. **Balancing Public Morality vs. Free Expression** â€“ â€œ[A]n undifferentiated fear or apprehension of disturbance is not enough.â€ Regulation must fit within narrow historical exceptions; Â§â€¯415 sweeps too broadly.

**Blackmun Dissent**
- Labels episode â€œabsurd and immature anticâ€ closer to conduct than speech; courthouse merits heightened civility; sees fit under _Chaplinsky_.

**Doctrinal Significance**
- ==**â€œOffensivenessâ€ â‰  unprotected**:== creates robust shield for profanity in public discourse.
- Clarifies limits of captiveâ€‘audience doctrine; seeds later cases (_FCCâ€¯v.â€¯Pacifica_ limits broadcast, but distinguishes Cohenâ€™s political context).
- Emphasizes **viewpoint and content neutrality**: state cannot punish because listeners are offended.
- Distinction cemented between **obscenity** (sexual content) and **indecency/profanity** (linguistic offensiveness).

**Ripple Effects & Modern Use**
- Cited whenever governments attempt to ban bumper stickers, band tâ€‘shirts, or protest signs with profanity (_Cityâ€¯ofâ€¯Erieâ€¯v.â€¯Papâ€™s A.M._, 2000; _Iancuâ€¯v.â€¯Brunetti_, 2019 trademark case).
- Forms backbone of campusâ€‘speech guidelines: emotional, even vulgar dissent is constitutionally sacrosanct.
- Internet era: platforms generally tolerating expletives rely on Cohenâ€™s rationale for nonâ€‘erotic profanity.

**Classroom Hotâ€‘Seat Questions**
- _Q: Could a judge expel Cohen from the courtroom itself?_ â†’ Possibly; courtroom is a nonâ€‘public forum with decorum rules, provided regulation is contentâ€‘neutral and narrowly tailored.
- _Q: What if Cohenâ€™s jacket targeted a person (â€œFâ€‘â€‘â€‘ Judgeâ€¯Smithâ€)?_ â†’ Moves toward **â€œtrue threatâ€ / fightingâ€‘words** territory; context matters.
- _Q: Do schools get Cohen protection?_ â†’ Not wholly; _Fraser_ (1986) allowed school discipline for lewd speech; studentsâ€™ rights not â€œcoextensiveâ€ with adults.
- _Q: Broadcast radio?_ â†’ _Pacifica_ (1978) allowed FCC to fine for midâ€‘day expletives due to pervasive/accessible medium and child audience.
- _Q: Hate slur instead of â€œFâ€‘â€‘â€‘â€?_ â†’ Still protected unless it is a **direct, personal insult** likely to provoke violence (_Chaplinsky_) or meets _Brandenburg_ incitement.

**Mnemonic**
> **â€œCohen = Coat + Curse â‰  Crime.â€**

**Bottom Line**  
The First Amendment shelters even raw, fourâ€‘letter political outbursts in public spaces. Government may regulate obscenity, threats, or direct incitementâ€”but **mere offensiveness is not a constitutional trump card**.

### 3. **_Bethelâ€¯Schoolâ€¯Districtâ€¯No.â€¯403â€¯v.â€¯Fraser_,â€¯478â€¯U.S.â€¯675â€¯(1986)**

â€” _Where the Court carved a Firstâ€‘Amendment â€œlewdâ€‘speechâ€ exception inside the schoolhouse gate_

**Factsâ€¯&â€¯Procedural Posture**
- **Aprilâ€¯26â€¯1983**â€¯â†’â€¯**Matthewâ€¯N.â€¯Fraser**, Bethel High School junior, delivers a 1â€‘minute nomination speech for a classmate at a **mandatory, schoolâ€‘sponsored assembly** (â‰ˆâ€¯600 students, many only 14). The speech brims with explicit sexual doubleâ€‘entendre (â€œfirm in his pantsâ€¦ to the very endâ€”the climaxâ€”for each and every one of youâ€).
- Reaction: laughter, catâ€‘callsâ€”**no fights, no mayhem**.
- Discipline: assistant principal cites Bethelâ€™s rule against â€œobscene, profane language or gestures,â€ suspends Fraser **three days** and **removes him from the commencementâ€‘speaker ballot**.
- **U.S.â€¯District Court (W.D.â€¯Wash.)** finds a **Â§â€¯1983 violation**, deems rule vague/overbroad, awards nominal damages.
- **Ninth Circuit (Tang,â€¯J.)** affirms: speech was political; **_Tinker_** governs; no material disruption.
- **SCOTUS** grants cert. **Novâ€¯12â€¯1985**, hears argument **Marâ€¯3â€¯1986**, decides **Julâ€¯7â€¯1986**.

**School Districtâ€™s Position**
- Schools inculcate civility; sexual vulgarity offends captive minors; educators act _in loco parentis_; rule targets **manner, not viewpoint**.

**Fraserâ€™s Position**
- Pure political endorsement; no obscenity; no significant disruption; rule void for vagueness/overbreadth; _Tinker_ protects absent material interference.

**Issues**
1. May publicâ€‘school officials punish a student for lewd, sexual metaphors at a compulsory assembly even without substantial disruption?
2. How does _Tinker_â€™s â€œmaterial and substantial interferenceâ€ test interact with vulgar, nonâ€‘political student speech?

**Holdingâ€¯&â€¯Vote**
- **7â€¯â€“â€¯2**. **Burger,â€¯C.J.** (joined by White, Powell, Rehnquist, Oâ€™Connor, Blackmun, Stevensâ€ ) upholds discipline; reverses Ninth Circuit.
- **Brennan** concurs in judgment.
- **Dissent**: **Stevens** (joined by Marshall).
- **Rule**: Public schools may sanction student speech that is **lewd, vulgar, or plainly offensive** and incompatible with the â€œfundamental values of publicâ€‘school education,â€ without proving _Tinker_â€‘level disruption.

**Majority Reasoning (Burger)**
1. **Civility Mission** â€“ Echoes Jefferson/Ambach: schools must teach â€œhabits and manners of civility.â€
2. **_Tinker_ Distinguished** â€“ Blackâ€‘armbands were silent political protest; Fraserâ€™s speech was intrusive sexual imagery in front of minors.
3. **Perâ€‘Se Lewdness Category** â€“ Vulgar sexual expression bears â€œslight social valueâ€ (_Pacifica_, _Chaplinsky_ analogies); schools get wider latitude than public streets.
4. **Captive Audience & _Inâ€¯Locoâ€¯Parentis_** â€“ Assembly forced attendance; 14â€‘yearâ€‘olds lacked realistic â€œavert the earâ€ option.
5. **No Viewpoint Discrimination** â€“ Sanction addressed **mode**, not Fraserâ€™s support for his candidate.
6. **Proportional Penalties** â€“ Threeâ€‘day suspension and lost speaking honor are educational, not punitive imprisonment.

**Brennan Concurrence**
- Discipline permissible _here_ because decorum expectations were clear; cautions against equating innuendo with obscenity; ==outside school, **_Cohen_** would control.==

**Stevensâ€“Marshall Dissent**
- **Vagueness/Overbreadth** â€“ Rule gave no fair notice that â€œmetaphorâ€ = â€œobscene.â€
- **No Real Disruption** â€“ Laughter â‰  material interference; punishment excessive.
- Courts shouldnâ€™t constitutionalize etiquette; schools can teach manners without suspensions.

**Doctrinal Significance**
- Establishes â€œ**_Fraser_ carveâ€‘out**â€: lewd or vulgar student speech is punishable **per se**, sidestepping _Tinker_.
- Forms second prong of the **tripartite studentâ€‘speech framework** later crystallized in _Hazelwood_ (curricular speech) and _Morse_ (drugâ€‘advocacy).

**Ripple Effectsâ€¯&â€¯Modern Use**
- Upholds bans on profanityâ€‘laden Tâ€‘shirts, sexually graphic posters, explicit rap lyrics in class projects.
- Digital era: circuits split on offâ€‘campus socialâ€‘media vulgarity; _Mahanoyâ€¯Areaâ€¯v.â€¯B.â€¯L._ (2021) limits _Fraser_ outside school grounds.
- Frequently cited in dressâ€‘code and cheerleaderâ€‘banner cases to justify discipline sans disruption evidence.

**Classroom Hotâ€‘Seat Questions**
- _Q: If Fraser had cursed school policy without sexual innuendo (â€œThis rule sucks!â€)?_ â†’ Likely political speech; many courts would require _Tinker_ disruption showing.
- _Q: Does _Fraser_ let schools ban â€œFâ€‘â€‘â€‘â€ on student clothing?_ â†’ Usually yes inside hallways; minors + captive audience tip scale.
- _Q: Valedictorian recites risquÃ© Shakespeare?_ â†’ If preâ€‘screened, school may excise under _Hazelwood_; if rogue live, postâ€‘hoc discipline likely under _Fraser_.
- _Q: Universities?_ â†’ Generally no; college students are adults; _Papish_ (1973) applies fuller Firstâ€‘Amendment scrutiny.

**Mnemonic**
> **â€œFraser = Filthy Figurative Flourish Forbidden in Front of Fellow Freshmen.â€**

**Bottom Line**  
Kâ€‘12 educators may insist on decorum and **punish lewd or sexually explicit student speech** even without proof of disruption. _Fraser_ narrows _Tinker_, carving a lewdâ€‘speech exception that anchors modern studentâ€‘expression doctrine.

| **Supreme Court Case**     | **What It Protects**                        | **What It Allows Government to Criminalize**   | **Effect on 18 U.S.C.**                                       |
| -------------------------- | ------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------- |
| **_Miller v. California_** | Speech that is not obscene                  | Obscene material meeting all 3 _Miller_ prongs | Limits all Â§1460â€“1467 applications to _Miller_ definition     |
| **_Cohen v. California_**  | Profane/offensive speech that is not erotic | Nothingâ€”offensive language alone is protected  | Narrows â€œindecentâ€ & â€œprofaneâ€ enforcement under Â§Â§â€¯1463â€“1464 |
| **_Fraser v. Bethel_**     | Political speech by students                | Lewd/vulgar speech in Kâ€“12 settings            | Justifies regulations in public schools, not criminal laws    |

## Week 7 Clarification Notes

### 1. â€œ **F*** Senator â€ shirt inside the Capitol **or** a courthouse

| **Setting**                                      | **Forum Type**                                           | **Baseline Rule**                                                                                                                                                             | **Likely Analysis**                                                                                                                                                               |
| ------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| **Public corridor / rotunda** (open to tourists) | Traditional or at least designated _public_ forum        | **Cohen** governs: profanity alone â‰  unprotected. Government may impose **time-place-manner** limits that are _content-neutral, narrowly tailored, alternative channels._     | Shirt is protected political speech; cannot be banned merely for vulgarity. Any rule singling out particular words would be _content-based_ and strict-scrutiny-fatal.            |
| **Senate gallery during session**                | _Limited_ public forum; decorum rules long-recognized.   | Rules of order may bar signs, chanting, or clothing with â€œdemonstrativeâ€ messages **so long as** they are _viewpoint-neutral_ and reasonable in light of the forumâ€™s purpose. | You can be expelled for violating the gallery dress code; but a blanket ban on criticism of senators while allowing â€œPro-Senatorâ€ garb would be invalid viewpoint discrimination. |
| **Inside a courtroom during proceedings**        | _Non-public_ forum; judge holds inherent contempt power. | Court may bar disruptive or disrespectful attire to preserve neutrality and decorum. **Nebulous â€œoffensivenessâ€ is enough here.**                                             | Likely permissible to eject or sanction; _Cohen_ yields to the judiciaryâ€™s stronger order-maintenance interest.                                                                   |

**Key doctrinal hooks**: _Cohen; Perry Educ. Assâ€™n; Madsen (court orders);_ courtroom contempt precedents. No obscenity (fails _Miller_ prongs), no fighting words (not a direct, face-to-face insult).

### 2. **â€œF*** Trump/President!â€ billboard on a pickup truck

- **Forum**: public streets â†’ traditional public forum for speech attached to private property.
- **Category**: core political viewpoint; not obscene, not fighting words (no personal addressee).
- **Rule**: Any enforcement (ticket for â€œobscenity,â€ zoningâ€sign ordinance, or disorderly-conduct statute) must be **content-neutral**. Cases challenging â€œF*** Bidenâ€ decals have uniformly favored the speaker because _Miller_ obscenity fails at the first prong (no appeal to prurient sexual interest).
- **Caveat**: A genuinely neutral size-or-traffic-safety sign ordinance (_Reed v. Town of Gilbert_) can require smaller lettering, but it cannot single out profanity or a viewpoint.

### 3. **â€œDisorderly-conductâ€ catch-alls** for loitering, drunkenness, public urination

|**Conduct**|**Speech?**|**1A Shield?**|**Bottom Line**|
|---|---|---|---|
|**Loitering / public intoxication**|Typically **non-expressive conduct**; _Oâ€™Brien_ symbolic-speech test rarely satisfied.|Minimal First-Amendment coverage.|Statutes OK if they have a clear mens rea and do not target a viewpoint (e.g., **City of Chicago v. Morales** struck down for vagueness, not for speech).|
|**Public urination / lewd exposure**|Pure conduct, or at most _low-value_ indecent speech.|Even if construed as expression, it would likely fit the _secondary effects_ / public-health rationaleâ€”highly regulable.|State can criminalize without implicating _Cohenâ€“Miller_ concerns.|
|**â€œDisorderly conductâ€ applied to chanting, picketing, swearing**|**Speech**; triggers scrutiny.|Must satisfy _Brandenburg_ (incitement) or _Chaplinsky_ (fighting words) or be a valid **time-place-manner** rule.|Mere profanity in public (without imminent violence) is protected; vague â€œoffensivenessâ€ provisions are void (_Coates_).|

### 4. **Library book curation and removal**

- **Public libraries** are viewed as **limited public fora** devoted to the communication of speech. Under _Board of Educ. v. Pico_ and circuit cases:
    - **Content-based but viewpoint-neutral** weeding (outdated science texts, duplicate copies) is permissible.
    - **Viewpoint discrimination** (removing all pro-LGBTQ titles, or exclusively â€œanti-colonialâ€ works) is presumptively unconstitutional.
    - Schools get slightly more leeway than public libraries, but even so, political viewpoints may not be excised solely for being â€œinappropriate.â€

### 5. **Rainbow flags; â€œAllahu Akbar!â€; â€œFrom the river to the seaâ€; â€œImpeach him,â€ etc.**

| **Phrase / Symbol**                                                                                                                                                           | **Possible Government Interest**     | **Relevant Category**                                                                                                  | **Likely Outcome**                                                            |
| ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Rainbow flag ðŸ³ï¸â€ðŸŒˆ                                                                                                                                                           | offense to certain religious parents | Pure political expression; not obscene or threatening                                                                  | Protected; restriction would be viewpoint discrimination.                     |
| â€œAllahu Akbarâ€ shouted in airport                                                                                                                                             | Public-safety concern                | Could morph into a **true threat** only if directed & contextual cues show intent to intimidate (_Virginia v. Black_). | Absent threatening context, protected religious speech.                       |
| â€œFrom the river to the seaâ€¦â€ on campus                                                                                                                                        | Alleged incitement                   | Apply **Brandenburg**: advocacy of violence must be **intended and likely** to produce imminent lawless action.        | Generally protected unless paired with explicit calls for immediate violence. |
| Calling government **â€œsexist, racist, colonial, fascistâ€**                                                                                                                    | Offensiveness                        | Core political speech; highest protection.                                                                             | Cannot be punished unless it meets another exclusion.                         |
| Parodying Trumpâ€™s insults                                                                                                                                                     | Political hyperbole                  | _Hustler v. Falwell_ â€“ rhetorical ridicule is protected; _Watts_ â€“ political hyperbole â‰  true threat.                  | Protected.                                                                    |

#### 5.5 Auto-Felon Quartet:

| â€œ**F*** these politicians/activists! _Someone_ really ought to blow up the office building / HBCU / Black church!â€ | **Advocacy of violence â†’ Brandenburg incitement test.** Speech is **protected** unless (i) **intended** to spur _imminent_ lawless action **and** (ii) objectively **likely** to do so. Vague â€œsomeone shouldâ€ + no timetable = usually _not_ imminent. | _Brandenburg v. Ohio_; _Hess v. Indiana_ (1973) (future-tense advocacy protected). Possible federal scrutiny under **18 U.S.C. Â§ 875(c)** (interstate threat) or hate-crime investigations.                                                     | Probably protected, but expect FBI knock & grand-jury subpoena if posted online; prosecution only if facts show concrete, imminent plot. |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| â€œ**I will bomb** the White House / plane / school / mall / office building!â€                                       | **True threat**â€”speaker expresses a _serious_ intent to commit violence. Not protected.                                                                                                                                                                 | _Virginia v. Black_ (2003); _Watts v. U.S._ (1969) (political hyperbole line). **18 U.S.C. Â§ 871** (Pres./VP threats), **Â§ 844(e)** (bomb threats by any medium), **Â§ 875(c)** (interstate threats), **Â§ 115** (threat against U.S. officials). | Felony indictment almost automatic; no need to prove ability to carry it out. Secret Service or ATF leads the arrest.                    |
| â€œI will bomb ___ on **XX date, XX time**.â€                                                                         | **True threat + hoax bomb threat.** Specific time makes it credible â†’ triggers evacuations.                                                                                                                                                             | Same as above **plus** **18 U.S.C. Â§ 1038(a)** (terroristic-hoax statute) and state â€œfalse reportâ€ laws.                                                                                                                                        | Felony; sentencing higher because emergency services are mobilized; restitution for evacuation costs common.                             |
| â€œThanks to **X**, Iâ€™ve built a **[named] bomb** and will deploy it at ___ on XX date, XX time.â€                    | **True threat + confession of terrorist conspiracy / material support.** No First-Amendment shelter.                                                                                                                                                    | **18 U.S.C. Â§ 2332a** (use of WMD), **Â§ 2332b** (terrorism transcending national boundaries), **Â§ 371** (conspiracy), **Â§ 842(p)** (instruction on explosives), **Â§ 2339A/B** (material support).                                               | Immediate arrest on multiple federal counts; potential life sentence. Speech itself is the overt act proving conspiracy.                 |

#### Special federal protection for high officials

- **18 U.S.C. Â§ 871** â€“ threats, knowingly and willfully â€œto kill, kidnap, or inflict bodily harmâ€ upon the President, Vice President, or those in the line of succession.
- **18 U.S.C. Â§ 879** â€“ extends similar protection to former Presidents, major candidates, and their families.
- **18 U.S.C. Â§ 1751** â€“ makes actual assaults on the President/VP a separate federal offense.

#### When you say â€œI will bomb the White House:â€

- You're arrested under **18 U.S.C. Â§ 871** before lunch.
- Your computer and phone are seized by federal agents.
- A prosecutor gets an indictment _without ever applying Brandenburg_.
- You sit in a cell unless you can afford bail, which is often denied in threat cases.
- If you fight it, you're spending **$100,000+** and 2+ years hoping a judge finds it wasn't a â€œtrue threat.â€

> ==Clearly, while one may freely engage in discussions about the application of these constitutional tests and principles in the classroom setting, in reality, ANY relevant speech can result in immediate felony charges.==

### 6. **Broadcast-specific indecency regulation**

- **FCC v. Pacifica (1978)** recognized a **narrow, medium-specific** carve-out for **â€œindecentâ€** (not obscene) speech on broadcast radio/television during hours when children are likely to be listening (the â€œsafe-harborâ€ rule).
- Rationale: **pervasiveness + easy accessibility to children**.
- **Cable, the Internet, streaming** â†’ protected platforms (_Reno v. ACLU_, _U.S. v. Playboy_)â€”Pacifica does **not** extend automatically.

### 7. **K-12 schools v. colleges**

|**Case Rule**|**Applies Where**|**Standard**|
|---|---|---|
|**Tinker (1969)**|Student political speech that is neither vulgar nor school-sponsored|School must show **â€œmaterial and substantial disruption.â€**|
|**Fraser (1986)**|Lewd, sexually explicit student speech on campus|Per se punishable; no Tinker disruption showing required.|
|**Hazelwood (1988)**|School-sponsored curricular speech (newspaper, play)|Educators may control so long as decision is _reasonably related to legitimate pedagogical concerns._|
|**Morse (2007)**|Student speech promoting illegal drug use at a school event|School may suppress to protect health/safety.|
|**Mahanoy (2021)**|Off-campus social-media speech|School authority â€œdiminishedâ€; Tinker factors apply but with greater weight to student autonomy.|
|**Universities**|Adults; _Papish (1973)_; _Rosenberger_|School may not employ Fraser-style lewdness rules; full public-forum analysis.|

### 8. **Government-employee & teacher speech**

1. **Pickering v. Board of Education (1968)**  
    _Outside_-duty, public-concern speech â†’ apply **Pickering balancing** (employee interest vs. workplace efficiency).
2. **Connick v. Myers (1983)**  
    _If_ the speech is **purely private grievance**, employer may discipline without First-Amendment scrutiny.
3. **Garcetti v. Ceballos (2006)**  
    Speech **made pursuant to official job duties** is **not** protected at all.  
    _Professorial academic freedom_ caveat left undecided; lower courts split.

Teachers disciplining students may invoke **Fraser/Hazelwood** controls, but their own speech outside class about politics is largely Pickering-protected.

### 9. **Putting it all together â€“ the decision tree**

1. **Is it speech or conduct?**  
    â€“ Pure conduct (urination, intoxication) â†’ ordinary police power.  
    â€“ Expressive conduct â†’ apply _Oâ€™Brien_ or _Johnson_.
2. **Does it fit a categorical exclusion?**  
    â€“ _Miller_ obscenity, _Ferber_ child porn, _Brandenburg_ incitement, _Chaplinsky_ fighting words, **true threat** (_Virginia v. Black_), defamation, fraud.
3. **Is there a context-specific carve-out?**  
    â€“ **Schools** (Tinker/Fraser/Hazelwood/Morse/Mahanoy)  
    â€“ **Broadcast** (Pacifica)  
    â€“ **Government employee** (Pickering/Garcetti)  
    â€“ **Courtroom / legislative floor** (non-public forum decorum)  
    â€“ **Prison/military** (very deferential Turner/Oâ€™Brien balancing)
4. **If none of the above,** apply ordinary forum analysis and _strict scrutiny_ to content-based restrictions, _intermediate_ to time-place-manner rules.
level of scrutiny (3-5), forum (5 types), type of speech (true threat, incitement, fighting words, obscenity), two modes of challeneg, types of restrictions (as applied, subject matter, viewpoiint), level of protection (proteced, less-protected, unprotected)

Must track **(1) category of speech**, **(2) type of restriction**, **(3) posture (facialâ€¯vsâ€¯asâ€‘applied)**

### Mnemonic Capsule

> **â€œCOHEN unless MILLER, BLACK, or BRANDENBURG takes it down, and FRASER or PACIFICA trims it in special places.â€**

That single sentence encodes:  
_General protection (Cohen), lost only to the big categorical exclusions (Miller obscenity; Black true threats; Brandenburg incitement), and trimmed by special-venue carve-outs (Fraser schools; Pacifica broadcast)._

## Week 7.2 â€” Conduct as Speech

### 1. **_United States v. Oâ€™Brien_, 391 U.S. 367 (1968)**

â€” _Warren Court forges the four-part â€œOâ€™Brien testâ€ for laws that incidentally burden expressive conduct_

**Facts & Procedural Posture**
- **Mar 31 1966 â†’ South Boston, MA:** **David Paul Oâ€™Brien**, 19 years old, and three companions publicly burn their ==Selective-Service draft cards== on the steps of the federal courthouse before a crowd (including FBI agents).
- Crowd attacks them; an agent brings Oâ€™Brien inside for safety. Oâ€™Brien admits purpose was to persuade others to resist the draft.
- Charged under the **1965 amendment to Â§ 12(b)(3) of the Universal Military Training & Service Act** (50 U.S.C. App. Â§ 462(b)(3)) â€” which, after 1965, ==criminalized _knowingly destroying_ a registration certificate.==
- **U.S. District Court (D. Mass.)** convicts; **First Circuit** affirms.
- **SCOTUS** grants cert. Nov 13 1967; argued Jan 24 1968; decided **May 27 1968**.

**Governmentâ€™s Theory**
- Draft cards are indispensable administrative tools; statute is ==content-neutral housekeeping==; disturbance to Selective-Service machinery justifies prohibition.

**Oâ€™Brienâ€™s Theory**
1. Burning was ==**symbolic speech** protesting the war== â‡’ First Amendment shields the act.
2. Statuteâ€™s ==â€œpurposeâ€ was to stifle anti-war expression== â‡’ unconstitutional content-based suppression.
3. Law is ==overbroad/vague==; less restrictive alternatives exist (fine for failure to possess card, etc.).

**Issues Presented**
1. Does the First Amendment protect destruction of draft cards as symbolic speech?
2. What standard governs statutes that penalize conduct with both speech and non-speech elements?
3. Was Â§ 462(b)(3) enacted to silence expression, rendering it unconstitutional?

**Holding & Vote**
- **8 â€“ 1** (Warren C.J.; joined by Black, Harlan, Brennan, Stewart, White, Fortas, Marshall).
- **Conviction affirmed**: statute is constitutional as applied.
- **Douglas** dissents (absolutist view: speech + conduct inseparable; law suppresses dissent).

**Core Reasoning (Warren, C.J.)**
1. **Definitional move** â€” Not every conduct labeled â€œspeechâ€ gets full immunity.
2. **Hybrid-conduct framework** â€” When _speech_ and _non-speech_ elements are â€œcombined,â€ government may regulate the _non-speech_ portion if four criteria are satisfied:
    > **(1)** within constitutional power; 
    > **(2)** furthers an _important/substantial_ governmental interest; 
    > **(3)** interest is _unrelated to suppression of expression_; 
    > **(4)** incidental restriction on First-Amendment freedoms is _no greater than essential._
    â†’ **The â€œOâ€™Brien test.â€**
3. **Application**
    - Congress has sweeping Art. I power to raise armies & administer Selective-Service.
    - Protecting cardsâ€™ availability is a substantial administrative interest.
    - Interest is _content-neutral_ â€” ==law bars all destruction, pro- or anti-war.==
    - No narrower means will assure ready access to cards.
4. **Legislative motive** â€” Even if some floor statements reflected hostility to dissent, valid broad purposes control absent proof of dominant censorship motive.

**Douglas Dissent**
- Draft cards are â€œidentification papers of the kind ==so typical of totalitarian regimes.==â€
- Burning is _pure speech_ indistinguishable from uttered protest; majority trivializes expressive element.

**Doctrinal Takeaways**
- **Oâ€™Brien test = ==intermediate scrutiny** for content-neutral laws regulating expressive conduct.==
- Distinguishes from _Texas v. Johnson_ category (content-based suppression) and from _Miller_ obscenity.
- Valid even where incidental burden on expression is foreseeable.
- Legislative motive rarely fatal if statute is facially neutral.

**Impact & Legacy**
- Forms backbone of _symbolic-speech_ analysis (flag-desecration, nude-dancing, draft-card possession, mask bans).
- Reappears in _Ward v. Rock Against Racism_ (1989) & _Turner Broad. v. FCC_ (1994) as template for tailoring inquiry.
- Modern security-credential laws (airport IDs, badge destruction) routinely survive under Oâ€™Brien.

**Socratic Traps & Answers**
- _Q: Could Congress instead criminalize private flag-burning under Oâ€™Brien?_ â†’ No; ==purpose of reverence for the flag is **content-related**== â†’ strict scrutiny (_Johnson_).
- _Q: If Oâ€™Brien had worn the card as an armband?_ â†’ Mere display â‰  destruction â‡’ statute not triggered; would fall under pure speech (strict scrutiny for any content-based ban).
- _Q: Does Â§ 3 interest survive after the draft ends?_ â†’ Court defers to Congressâ€™s administrative judgment; future mootness irrelevant to 1968 conviction.

**Mnemonic**

> **O-B-R-I-E-N** = **O**nly **B**urden when **R**easonable, **I**mportant, **E**xpressive-Neutral, **N**arrow.

**Bottom Line**  
_Oâ€™Brien_ legitimizes content-neutral regulation of expressive conduct via the **four-part test**, anchoring modern intermediate scrutiny for symbolic-speech cases.

### 2. **_Tinker v. Des Moines I.C.S.D._, 393 U.S. 503 (1969)**

â€” _Fortas opinion declares that students do not â€œshed their constitutional rights at the schoolhouse gate,â€ birthing the â€œmaterial-and-substantial disruptionâ€ test_

**Facts & Procedural Posture**
- **Dec 1965 â†’ Des Moines, Iowa:** siblings **Mary Beth Tinker** (13) & **John Tinker** (15) and friend **Chris Eckhardt** (16) ==plan to wear **black armbands** during Advent to mourn Vietnam war dead and advocate a truce.==
- School principals adopt a pre-emptive rule: remove armband or face suspension until compliance.
- Students wear bands; silently attend class; face peer comments but no violence. Five suspended.
- Parents sue under 42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983; **U.S. D.C. (S.D. Iowa)** upholds school; **Eighth Circuit** affirms by ==equally divided en banc court.==
- **SCOTUS** cert. granted Nov 12 1968; argued Nov 12; decided **Feb 24 1969**.

**School Districtâ€™s Position**
- Armbands provoke controversy during wartime; forecast of disturbance justified preventive discipline; schools can prescribe dress and conduct.

**Studentsâ€™ Position**
- ==Silent, passive== political expression = pure speech; no evidence of ==disruption==; policy singles out Vietnam opposition â‡’ ==viewpoint== discrimination.

**Issues Presented**
1. Are silent armbands in school protected speech under the First & Fourteenth Amendments?
2. What standard governs school regulation of student expression?

**Holding & Vote**
- **7 â€“ 2** (Fortas, Brennan, White, Marshall, Douglas, Stewart, Warren â‡¢ majority).
- **Discipline unconstitutional; suspensions reversed.**
- **Black & Harlan** dissent (Black joined by Harlan).

**Core Reasoning (Fortas, J.)**
1. **Students are â€œpersonsâ€ under the Constitution.** ==They do not abandon rights at the schoolhouse gate.==
2. **Symbolic armband = â€œpure speech.â€** Historically, Barnette flag-salute and Meyer language-teaching protect student/teacher expression.
3. **Standard articulated** â€” School may regulate only where expression would ==**â€œmaterially and substantially disruptâ€**== classwork or invade othersâ€™ rights; mere ==â€œundifferentiated fearâ€== or desire to avoid discomfort is insufficient.
4. **Application** â€” Record shows no disruption; policy singled out Vietnam armbands while allowing other political symbols â‡’ viewpoint discrimination.
5. Tolerance of controversy is essential to democratic education; suppression cultivates â€œtotalitarian enclaves.â€

**Black Dissent**
- Predicts erosion of discipline; courts usurp local control; students are in school to learn, not teach; any deviation distracts.

**Harlan Dissent**
- Would uphold rule absent proof of â€œirrationalityâ€; courts should defer to reasonable professional judgment of educators.

**Doctrinal Takeaways**
- **â€œTinker testâ€**: speech protected unless school proves ==_material/substantial disruption_ or invasion of rights.==
- Distinguishes later **_Fraser_** (lewd speech), **_Hazelwood_** (school-sponsored curriculum), **_Morse_** (drug advocacy), **_Mahanoy_** (off-campus).
- Establishes viewpoint-neutrality requirement in K-12 fora.

**Impact & Legacy**
- Backbone of student-speech jurisprudence; cited in social-media cases, LGBTQ armbands, climate-strike walkouts, Black Lives Matter shirts.
- Post-Columbine & Parkland walkouts reignited debates over â€œdisruption.â€
- International influence: echoed in Canadian, Indian, and European education-rights rulings.

**Socratic Traps & Answers**
- _Q: Would a walkout be protected?_ â†’ Likely disruptive â†’ school may discipline under Tinker.
- _Q: Confederate-flag shirts?_ â†’ Evaluate disruption & harassment of protected classes; many circuits uphold bans if racial tension evidence exists.
- _Q: Off-campus Snapchat rant?_ â†’ _Mahanoy_: schools have reduced authority; must prove substantial disruption with greater burden.

**Mnemonic**

> **â€œTINKER = **T**eachers **I**mpose? **N**o, **K**idsâ€™ **E**xpression **R**emainsâ€”unless real disruption.â€**

**Bottom Line**  
_Tinker_ cements robust First-Amendment rights for K-12 students: _silent, non-lewd political expression_ cannot be punished absent a _specific, evidence-based forecast_ of substantial disruption or rights invasion.

|**Supreme Court Case**|**Rule / Test Announced**|**Speech Unprotected**|**Sphere of Application**|
|---|---|---|---|
|**_Oâ€™Brien_** (1968)|Four-part **Oâ€™Brien test** for **content-neutral** regulation of expressive _conduct_|Conduct whose incidental burden satisfies the 4 parts|Draft-card destruction, generally symbolic conduct|
|**_Tinker_** (1969)|**Material-&-substantial disruption** standard for student speech|Only school expression causing real disruption or rights invasion|K-12 public schools (non-lewd, non-curricular)|
